Disclaimer

This  work  is  a  product  of  the  consultant  engaged  by  BPPA,  IMED,  Ministry  of  Planning.  The
findings,  interpretations,  and  conclusions  expressed  in  this  work  do  not  necessarily  reflect  the
views  of  the  government.  BPPA  does  not  guarantee  the  accuracy  of  the  data  included  in  this
publication  and  accepts  no  responsibility  for  any  consequence  of  their  use.  The  purpose  of  the
dissemination of this report is to support procuring entities and help them take remedial actions of
the observations/recommendations of the report. It also facilitates them to improve procurement
transactions. BPPA has all rights reserved of the document.

Executive Summary

Bangladesh  Public  Procurement  Authority  (BPPA)  under  Implementation  Monitoring  and
Evaluation Division (IMED), Ministry of Planning is mandated by PPA, 2006 and PPR, 2008 to
review procurement activities of any Procuring Entity (PE) for total procurement performed in a
financial  year  worth  at  least  taka  ten  (10)  cores.  Accordingly,  BPPA  has  appointed  this
Procurement  Post  Review  Consultant  to  review  thirty  (30)  procurement  contracts  from  five  (5)
PEs  of    Central  Medical  Stores  Depot  (CMSD)  executed  in  the  financial  year  2023-2024.  The
contracts were selected through in-depth consultation with BPPA and CMSD. Out of thirty (30)
contracts, twenty eight (28) were  goods and two (2) were service contracts. No works contracts
were found. All the contracts followed OTM method out of which eight (8) were NCT and twenty
two (22) were ICT. Six (6) contracts were processed through e-GP and the rest were manual.

To  achieve  the  objectives  and  perform  the  scope  of  services  of  the  assignment,  data  and
documents were collected through “Individual Contract Review Format” provided by the  BPPA
from  the  PEs.  On  the  basis  of  those  data  and  documents,  reviews  were  done  regarding  the
procurement  management  capacity  of  PEs;  contract-wise  competition;  contract-wise  time
compliance; contract-wise time overrun; contract-wise cost overrun;  contract-wise timeliness in
bill  payment;  contract-wise  audit  objections;  contract-wise  procurement  method;  contract-wise
committee  (TOC/TEC/OCEC)  formation;  physical  inspection  such  as  quality,  defects  etc.
Findings of the review have been shown in Chapter Three of the report.

The  review  revealed  that  CMSD  had  limited  management  capacity  for  procurement  due  to
shortage of manpower. As no Procurement Cell exists, the staff who had no procurement training,
could not  get  proper  guidance.  Procurement  documentation  was  maintained  properly. Tecnnical
specification and official cost estimate were prepared by the Technical Specification Committee
and Official Cost Estimate Committee respectively. Advertisements were made properly, pre-bid
meetings were held,  proper STDs were used. TOC and TEC were formed properly although these
were not approved by the proper authority. Tender security and performance security were as per
rules. Timeliness  in  contract  processing  namely    tender  submission, tender  opening,  evaluation,
approval, NoA issue, Contract Signing etc were pursued almost properly except few cases where
delay occurred in evaluation, approval and NoA issue. Although time overrun happened in most
of the cases, no cost overrun occurred. Goods supplied were verified by the “Technical Inspection
and Acceptance Committee (Survey Committee)”. No complaints and appeals were raised against
any contract and audit objections were minimal and not serious in nature.

However,  some  deficiencies  were  found  in  case  of  preparation  of  APP,  pursuit  of  procurement
method  and  type  and  follow  of    DoFP.  Low  participation  was  observed  coupled  with  low
responsiveness  and  abnormally  low  QP.  One  or  more  tenderers  winning  some  tenders  were
frequent.  Time  overrun  in  case  of  ICT  contracts  found  common.  Abnormal  delay  occurred    in
distribution of goods and in bill payment. Procurement of lower priced equipments and delay in
distribution caused some equipments to perform low. Although domestic suppliers took part in all
tenders, the floating of international tender did not seem appropriate.

ix

Against  this  backdrop,  recommendations  have  been  put  forward  to  enhance  the  management
capacity of CMSD by filling up vacant posts and imparting training to the staff. A Procurement
Cell  needs  to  be  set  up  to  give  guidance  to  the  procurement  related  personnel.  APP  should  be
prepared in the beginning of financial year and updated periodically. Indicative dates mentioned
in the APP should be abided by as far as possible. Technical Specification should be prepared in
such a way that will deter the low grade products. Proper procurement method and type should be
clearly mentioned in Operation Plan and APP. OCEC, TOC & TEC should be approved by the
proper authority mentioned in PPR. Time mentioned in Schedule III Part- ka of PPR, 2008 should
be pursued in processing of tenders. DoFP should be followed in case of approval of evaluation
report.  NoA  should  be  issued  within  one  week  of  approval  of  evaluation  report.  The  matter
relating to single tender selling, single tender submission, single responsive tender, too  much less
quoted  price  should  be  examined    properly  in  order  to  ensure  fair  competition.  One  or  more
suppliers winning more tenders should be carefully verified. Time overrun should be discouraged
and  allowed  in  special  circumstances  only.  Proper  verification  and  testing  of  goods  should  be
ensured so that low quality products are not received. Distribution of goods should be prompt and
bill  payment  should  be  made  timely.  As  no  international  tenderers  participate,  follow  of  NCT
instead of ICT could be contemplated.

Although  it  is  not  possible  to  comment  on  the  overall  procurement  performance  of  CMSD
reviewing only thirty contracts, but this review has unearthed the strengths and weaknesses in the
procurement process of CMSD. The purpose of this report is to facilitate CMSD to improve their
procurement  performance  by  overcoming  these  weaknesses.  It  is  hoped  that  the  findings  and
recommendations  put  forward  in  this  report  will  guide  CMSD  to  improve  their  procurement
performance.

x

Chapter -One

Introduction

1.1 Background and Context

Bangladesh has obtained Public Procurement reform since 2002, through establishing institutional
& legal frame work and institutional capacity building program simultaneously. During the last 21
years,  it  has  made  commendable  progress  in  building  foundation  for  its  public  procurement
system  within  the  purview  of  the  legal  framework  PPA,2006  and  PPR,2008.  As  a  part  of  the
institutional  reform  initiative,  Bangladesh  Public  Procurement  Authority  (BPPA)  has  been
established and it is mandated to implement the reform in order to ensure transparency, efficiency,
accountability to acquire value for money in public expenditure.

Section-24  of  the  PPA,2006  and  Rules  45  &  46  of  the  PPR,  2008,  entrusted  BPPA  with  the
responsibility  to  review  and  monitor  the  progress  of  implementation  of  the  PPA,2006  and
PPR,2008  in  procurement  management  in  government  agencies.  According  to  the  above  Rules,
the  records  of  procurement  activities  of  any  Procuring  Entity  (PE)  shall  be  reviewed  by  BPPA
using  own resources  by  hiring  Independent  Consultants if  the  total  procurement  performed  in a
financial  year  worth  at  least  taka  10.00  (Ten)  crore  in  a  PE.  As  per  Rule  46(7)  of  PPR,2008,
BPPA  may  conduct  procurement  post  review  of  the  contracts  of  PE  as  a  sample  basis.  The
Procuring Entity to be reviewed will be specified by the BPPA and the Independent Consultants
will verify the compliance of the provisions of the Act and Rules taken in procurement process.

In view of the above, BPPA has selected 13 (Thirteen) individuals as “Procurement Post Review
Consultant”  for  thirteen  different  organizations  to  review  the  selected  procurement  contracts
executed  during  financial  year  2023-2024.  The  author  was  selected  to  review  the  procurement
contracts of the Central Medical Stores Depot (CMSD), an  agency of  the Directorate General of
Health  Services  (DGHS)  of  the  Health  Services  Division  (HSD)  under  Ministry  of  Health  and
Family  Welfare  (MoHFW),  Government  of  Bangladesh,  responsible  for  procurement,  storage,
distribution  of  medical  and  surgical  equipments,  medicines  and  chemicals,  hospital  machinery
and other materials for public hospitals and agencies or offices under DGHS and HSD. Contract
Agreement with the BPPA was signed on 09 December 2024 in this regard.

1.2: Objectives of the Assignment

The  overall  objective  of  the  assignment  was  to  ensure  efficient,  effective  and  economic  use  of
resources in the public procurement by checking the compliance of the provisions of PPA, 2006
and  PPR,  2008  as  well  as  the  development  partners‟  guidelines/regulations  in  the  procurement
process  through  reviewing  the  records  and  other  necessary  documents  related  to  the  sample
contracts and thereby, ensuring value for money in the public procurement transactions.

The specific objectives were to:

1

(a)

(b)

(c)

(d)

(e)

(f)

(g)

(h)

determine  whether  the  procurement  processes  and  contracting  proceedings
were in accordance with the PPA, 2006 and PPR, 2008;
review whether official cost estimate had been prepared as per PPR, 2008 and
whether it was modified by the approving authority and opened as per rules;
review  whether  STD  was  followed  &  tender  document  is  prepared  in  line
with the act and rules;
assess the extent to which Value for Money(VfM) was being achieved in the
procurement process as required by the ProcuringEntities;
review  and  assess  the  capacity  of  the  Procuring  Entity  in  handling
procurement from preparation to contract implementation and documentation
were maintained as per Rules and required standards;
review  and  assess  that  the  procurements  executed  were  being  used  for  the
intendedpurpose;
identify  improvements  in  the  procurement  contracting  in  the  light  of
deficiencies identified in the earlier review; and
any other issues that might appear pertinent.

1.3: Scope of the Assignment

(i)  The  procurement  process  of  selected  Procuring  Entity  would  be  reviewed  by  the
Consultant if the total procurement performed in a financial year worth at least taka 10.00
crore  or  it  might  be  as  per  Rule  46(7)  of  PPR,  2008  on  sample  basis.  The  Individual
Consultant  would  review  the  procurement  contracts  completed/awarded  in  the  financial
year 2023-2024. The Consultant would review and evaluate six (6) procurement contracts
from each of five (5) Procuring Entities. The consultant would-

a.  verify  the  procurement  and  contracting  proceeding  executed  by  the  procuring

entity and identify non-compliance with the procurement Act and Rules;

b.  verify  whether  PE has used  Standard Tender  Documents  (STDs)  and  Standard
Request  for  Proposals  (SRfPs)  in  its  Procurement  content  and  put  necessary
information in Tender Data Sheet (TDS), Proposal Data Sheet (PDS) and other
formats correctly;

c.  verify, to the extent possible, whether the Goods, Works and Services contracted
required

were  supplied/performed  and/or  completed  according
specifications and technical standards;
identify inappropriate practices or questionable decisions and/or actions;

d.
e.  verify whether documentation and record keeping systems  that are in place are

the

to

adequate for ensuring post review requirements;

f.  verify  whether  goods  and  works  existed  at  intended  locations  and  were  being

used for the purposes they were acquired;

g.  determine remedies to correct the identified deficiencies;

(ii)  For each contract package, the consultant would review the following key elements:

a.  Procurement plan, budget, official costestimate;
b.  Procurement  type, category,  method,  objective, financing  arrangement  (in  case

of DP‟s supported project);

d.  Tender/proposal  submissions  (number  of  sale/issuance  of  tender/proposal
documents, no of participants, days allows for preparation andsubmission);
e.  Formation of TOC/POC and TEC/PEC (number of members, externalmembers);

responsiveness  (responsive  &  non-responsive),  recommendations  of

r.  Audit objection and status.

1.4 Methodology

The procurement post review has followed four (4) broad phases as stated below:

Phase-I: Selection of Sample Procurement Contracts

a.  Meeting  with  the  PEs  of  five  (5)  selected  Operation  Plans  and  discuss  with  them

about the objectives of the procurement post review;
b.  Obtaining lists of the procurement contracts from the PEs;
c.  Selection of  thirty (30) procurement contracts completed in FY 2023-24 from five
(5)  OPs  taking  care  of  the  main  considerations  provided  by  the  BPPA  as  broad
guidelines for selection.

Phase-II: Data and Documents Collection

a.  Collection  of  procurement  related  data  and  documents  of  thirty  (30)  procurement
contracts  from  five  (5)  Procuring  Entities  in  the  light  of    format  provided  by  the
BPPA;

3

b. b.  Visit to implementation sites and physical inspection in the light of format provided

by the BPPA.

c.  Phase-III: Documentary Review and Data Analysis

a.  Study and analyse the data and information obtained from PE offices and field visits

as per BPPA guidelines;

b. a.  Data processing: compilation, analysis etc.

Phase-IV: Report preparation & Submission

a.  Preparation and submission of Inception Report ;
b.  Preparation and submission of Interim Progress Report ;
c.  Preparation and submission of Draft Final Report in the light of format provided by

the BPPA;

d.  Organizing local and national workshops in cooperation with BPPA;
e.
f.

Incorporation of workshop‟s recommendations in the Final Report;
Submission of Final Report.

1.5 Limitations

This report has been prepared based on review of 30 contracts from 5 OPs of CMSD executed in
FY  2023-24.  These  30  contracts  have  total  spending  of  65  crore  taka  only.  CMSD  signed  50
contracts in FY 2022-23 amounting to TK 248 crore and 18 contracts in FY 2023-24 amounting
to TK 62 crore. Most of the contracts signed in FY 2022-23 and FY 2023-24 have been completed
and  30  contracts  were  selected  from  these  contracts.  So,  the  sample  of  30  contracts  is  a  small
representation  and  based  on  these  it  would  be  difficult  to  make  a  comment  on  the  overall
procurement performance of the CMSD.

1.6  Observations/Views/Comments/Recommendations  of  the  Workshop  and  its
Compliance:

The Draft Final Report was submitted to the BPPA and it was placed before local workshop held
on 17 April 2025 and national workshop held on 19 May 2025. On the basis of the Report of the
Rapporteurs  of  the  national  workshop,  the  relevant  issues  have  been  revised,  included  and
corrected in this Final Report. The checklist of compliance is attached at Annex-III (page: 266 ).

4

Chapter -Two

Selection of Projects and Contracts for Review

2.1   Selection of Projects

According  to  the  ToR,  the  IC  has  been  entrusted  to  review  six  (6)  procurement  contracts  from
each of five (5) Procuring Entities. For this reason, BPPA requested CMSD to send a list of those
pojects which have total procurement performed in FY2023-24 worth taka 5.00 crore or above. It
is to be mentioned that CMSD does not implement any project. It carries out procurement under
various Operation Plans (OPs) of Health Population and Nutrition Sector Program (HPNSP). 4th
HPNSP was started in January 2017 and ended in June 2024. It consisted of 29 Operation Plans.
So  all  procurements  were  carried  out  under  4th  HPNSP.  CMSD  carried  out  procurement  in  FY
2023-24 under the following Operation Plans:

2.2  Limitations in selecting projects (OPs)

CMSD carried out procurement activities under eight (8) Operation Plans. But ten (10) crore or
more  taka  was  not  spent  in  all  Operation  Plans  in  FY  2023-24  and  all  Operation  Plans  did  not
have  six(6)  or  more contracts  completed in  FY  2023-24.  So,  some  Operation  Plans  which  have
not  spent  ten(10)  crore  taka  had  to  be  selected.  Similarly  some  Operation  Plans  which  did  not
have  six(6)  completed  contracts  in  FY  2023-24  had  to  be  selected.  So,  choice  for  selecting
projects(OPs) was limited.

2.5 Limitations in selecting contracts

In selecting the sample contracts, due considerations were supposed to be given to the following
criteria :

  Category: Goods, Works and Services contracts;
  Value of the contracts: low value, medium value, high value;
  Method of procurement: OTM/LTM/…………
  Type: NCT/ICT;
  Budget: Revenue budget/development budget;
  Source of Fund: GOB/ DPs;
  Size:  Small/simple, small/less complex, more elaborate/complex;

13

Contracts based on tendering system (e-GP & manual)641Total no. of contracts=47Total no. of contracts based on e-GP systemTotal no. of contracts based on manual system624Contracts selected for review=30No. of contracts based on e-GP system selectedNo. of contracts based on manual system selected31

  Pre-qualification/ Post-qualification;
  Method of selection of consultants: SSS/ QCBS…..
  Location of implementation: remoteness of site;
  Status  of  work:  contract  signed  in  FY  2023-24  and  completed  in  FY  2023-24/  contract
signed in FY 2023-24 and ongoing/contract signed before FY 2023-24 and completed in
FY 2023-24

However, due to some limitations mentioned below all the above criteria could not be followed
fully.

CMSD did not execute works contracts. It only dealt with goods procurement during the review
period.  Only  two  service  procurements  for  carrying  goods  were  found.  It  followed  only  OTM
method  of  procurement,  no  other  methods  were  found.  As  it  carried  out  procurement  under
HPNSP  sector  program,  there  were  no  separate  procurement  under  revenue  and  development
budget and source of fund was both GOB/DP. Three OPs (TBL-ASP, PSSM-HS and MNCAH)
did not have six(6) contracts completed in FY 2023-24, some were completed in FY 2024-25.  So
the contracts completed in FY 2024-25 had to be selected.

14

Chapter -Three

Review of Contracts: Data Analysis and Findings

3.1 Procedure followed in  Post Review of Contracts

For  every  contracts  detail  information  has  been  collected  through  filling  up  the  formats-
„Individual Contract Review Format (including General information & compliance of PPA/PPR‟;
„Post  Reviews  of  Goods/Works  Contracts‟;  „Report  on  Physical  Inspection‟  which  has  been
produced in detailed form  in Annex-I in a chronological order (one contract after another). The
data and documents collected from the PE offices as well as from the LD offices were reviewed
intensively. Face to face/on-line discussion with the concerned persons were held several times to
collect the documents/information & to verify the information. Physical visit  was carried out to
review/validate the information/data and to review the quality of goods.

On the basis of those data and documents, the following reviews were done:

  Review of Procurement Management Capacity of PE (CMSD)
  Manpower Strength, Training on PPR/e-GP, Work load etc;
  Procurement Mechanism in CMSD;
  Functioning of Procurement Cell in CMSD;
  Compliance of Delegation of financial power in CMSD;
  Preparation of Procurement Plan.
  Review of Procurement proceedings of PE (CMSD)
  Review of Contract-wise Competition in procurements;
  Review of Contract-wise Time compliance;
  Review of Contract-wise Time overrun;
  Review of Contract-wise Cost overrun;
  Reviews of Timeliness in Bill Payment and Audit Objections;
  Review of Compliance with Procurement Method and Package;
  Review of Compliance with committee formation.
  Review of Physical Inspection: quality, defects observed.

3.2 Review of Procurement Management Capacity of CMSD

CMSD  is  an    agency  of    the  Directorate  General  of  Health  Services  (DGHS)  of  the  Health
Services  Division  (HSD)  under  Ministry  of  Health  and  Family  Welfare  (MoHFW).  It  is
responsible for procurement, storage, distribution of medical and surgical equipments, medicines
and chemicals, hospital machinery and other materials for public hospitals and agencies or offices
under DGHS and HSD. CMSD does not implement any project. It carries out procurement under
various  Operation  Plans  (OPs)  of  Health  Population  and  Nutrition  Sector  Program  (HPNSP).
Each OP has a Line Director (LD). Director, CMSD is the ex-officio Line Director of PSSM-HS
Operation  Plan.  Other  OPs  have  individual  Line  Directors  but  on  behalf  of  all  Line  Directors
CMSD acts as “Executing Agency” under Rule 12 of PPR and acts as PE. So all procedures of
procurement  are  done  by  the  CMSD.The  procurement  management  capacity  of  CMSD  has

15

been assessed on the following aspects:
(a) Availability of required manpower;
(b) Training on procurement Act & Rules and e-GP system;
( c)  Workload.

3.2.1 Procurement  M anagement  Capacity  of  CMSD

3.2.2 Findings on Procurement Management Capacity of PE (CMSD)

  Strength  of  manpower:  CMSD  is  suffering  from  shortage  of  manpower.  63%  of

manpower is vacant.

  Training on PPR/e-GP: Procurement related officers have training on procurement but

staff have no training. Staff are dealing with some procurement matters.

  Workload (Number of contracts and money spent): Although in FY 2023-24 workload

was not so heavy but in previous year it was heavy.

 3.3 Review of Procurement Mechanism in CMSD

As CMSD is working as “Executing Agency” as per Rule 12 of PPR, 2008 on behalf of various
LDs, it carries out procurement  and acts as PE. DG, DGHS is working as HOPE.  So Director,
CMSD prepares APP and gets it approved by the DG, DGHS. OCE is prepared by OCEC which
has been formed by the Health Services Division. TOC, TEC, Technical Committes and Goods
Acceptance Committee(Survey Committee) have also been formed by the HSD.

3.3.1 Findings on the review of Procurement Mechanism

 Centralised  Procurement  System:  Every  procurement  matter  is  processed  by  the
Director, CMSD(as PE) and approved by the DG, DGHS (as HOPE).

 Formation of OCEC and OCE: OCE is prepared by OCEC and OCEC is formed by the
HSD although PE is supposed to form it as per PPR.

  Preparation of Procurement Plan: Annual Procurement Plan was not prepared in the
beginning  of  each  financial  year  as  per  Rule-16(5)  of  PPR,  2008.  CMSD  after  getting
requisition from the LD Office prepared APP for each package and got it approved by the
HOPE. No proof of sending specific APP to the BPPA for publication in BPPA‟s website.

3.4 Procurement Cell in CMSD

There is no “Procurement Cell” in CMSD.

3.5 Delegation of Financial Power (DoFP) in CMSD

As per DoFP, 2015 issued by Ministry of Finance, PD of Class „Ka‟ category project (above 100
crore  taka)  is  authorized  to  approve  contracts  of  goods  upto  10  crore  taka.  The  amounts  are  5
crore taka for Class Kha (50-100 crore taka) and 3 crore taka for class Ga (upto 50 crore taka)
projects. This authority could have been exercised by the LDs of OPs. Although CMSD worked
as Executing Agency in case of 4 OPs under Rule 12 of PPR, in case of 1 OP Director CMSD was
the LD. But in all cases contracts were approved by the HOPE.

3.6 Review of Procurement proceedings during FY 2023-24

3.6.1  Review of Competition in Procurements

participated,  number  of  responsive  bidders  and  quoted  price  )  have  been  highlighted  in  the

3.6.2  Findings on the Competition in Procurements

  Number of participation: Low Participation. In 9 contracts, single tender was submitted
& accepted. In 4 contracts single tender document was sold. In 4 cases only re-invitation
was made. Although this is not unlawful but it indicates lack of competitiveness which is
contrary to the objective of the PPR. However, Low participation  & low QP  could not be
attributed to improper publication of advertisement and pre-bid meeting. It was stated that
some goods were produced or imported by a single organization. In this case DPM method
could have been followed instead of OTM. It would  save time and costs (as negotiation
could have been made).

  Responsive/Non-responsive:  Low  Responsiveness.  In  12  contracts,  single  tender  was
responsive and contract signed. Although this is not unlawful but it‟s not a good indication
of proper competition.

  OCE and Quoted Price: Some QP much below the OCE [G-2209(Lot-1) Freezer (Indian)
50.22% less]. This indicates the lack of proper preparation of OCE and specification. In
some  cases,  OCE  deemed  to  have  been  prepared  without  taking  into  consideration  the
market  price  or  OCE  was  prepared for  higher  grade  products  but  offer  was lower  grade
product. It might have impact on the quality of goods.

  Tender concentration: It was found that 16 contracts were awarded to 5 suppliers out of
which 1 supplier (GEMESCO) was awarded with 6 contracts, 1 supplier with 4 contracts
and 3 suppliers with 2 contracts each. The rest 14 contracts were awarded to 14 different
suppliers. So one or more tenderers winning a disproportionate number of contracts over
time was frequent.

3.6.4 Findings on the Contract-wise Time compliance

  Delay occurred in evaluation of 11 contracts  (37%)  and NOA issue of 1 contract (3%).
However,  Opening to NOA issue was delayed in 4 contracts (13%) and Opening to
Contract Signing in 2 contracts (7%) only.

  NOA issued within initial Tender Validity Period in all cases.
  Contract signed within 28 days of NOA issue in all cases.
  It indicates pursue of  timeliness in contract processing.

3.6.6 Findings on the Contract-wise Time Overrun

  Time overrun was not rampant in case of NCT contracts. Out of 8 NCT contracts only 3
had time overrun. However, in case of 22 ICT contracts 12 had time overrun. This was
due to delay in PSI and Shipment.

  In case of supplier‟s default, LD was imposed. In 10 cases LD was imposed(2 NCT & 8
ICT). In case of valid reasons time extension was approved by the proper authority.

3.6.8 Findings on the Contract-wise Costs

  OCE was lower than the APP cost;

cost variation.

3.6.14    Findings  on  the  Compliance  with  Procurement  Method,  and  Splitting  of
Packages

  Procurement  method  was  not  mentioned  in  OPs  and  APPs.  In  all  cases  OTM  was

followed.

  Procurement  type  was  mentioned  as  NCB/ICB  in  OPs.  But  in  APP  NCT  or  ICT  was

mentioned and tender was floated accordingly.

  Packages  mentioned  in  APP  were  not  in  consistence  with  the  packages  mentioned  in

OPs.

  Indicative  dates  mentioned  in  OPs  were  changed  in APP  and  dates  mentioned  in  APP

could not be abided by.

  APP was approved by the HOPE (DGHS).

3.6.16  Findings on the Compliance with Committee Formation:

  OCEC  was  formed  in  all  contracts.  But  it  was  formed  by  the  HSD  although
OCEC was supposed to be prepared by the PE as per Rule 16(5Ka) of PPR, 2008.
  TOC  and  TEC  were  formed  by  the  HSD  although  TOC  was  supposed  to  be
approved by HOPE as per Rule 7 and TEC was supposed to be approved by the
Approving Authority as per Rule 8 of PPR, 2008.

  Director,  CMSD  was  made  Chair  of  OCEC,  TOC  and  Technical  Specification

Preparation Committee which was not deemed appropriate.

   However,  number  of  members  of the TOC,  TEC  and  OCEC  were  as  per  PPR,

2008.

3.7.1  Findings on the Physical Inspections
  Maximum goods are lying with the CMSD store.
  Goods located at CMSD store were physically verified. Some  goods distributed to various

hospitals were verified.

  The quality  of  goods  could  not  be  ascertained.  All  goods  were  tested  by  various  technical

persons/institutions.

  Concerned Officials who were using the goods informed that the goods were working. But
they said that the quality of some goods especially equipments were not up to the mark.
  Delay occurred in distribution of goods. In some cases it was abnormal which had adverse
impact on the effectiveness of goods [Goods received on 13-11-23 of package no. G-2210
have not yet been distributed].

  Procurement  of  lower  priced  equipments  and  delay  in  distribution  might  cause  some

equipments to perform low.

3.8  Review of Compliance of previous year‟s recommendations: No review was done

before.

3.9.1  Findings on the VfM Indicators

  General: No contracts followed development partners rule. All followed PPR & e-gp

guidelines.

  Competition: Low Competition [average no. of tenders sold=6.27; tender

submitted=3.8; responsive tenders=2.77; percentage of tenders with at least 3
responsive tenders 40%; percentage of tenderers outside of PE‟s district=0%]
  Planning Efficiency: Small amount of contract value [Average 217.24 lakh taka]
  Performance Efficiency: Good [50% of contracts completed within original period

although payment was delayed in maximum cases]

  Complaints/Appeals: No complaints & appeals
  Implementation Efficiency: Good [50% completed within original period; average
time overrun for 15 contracts= 54 days; average cost overrun is nil; average no. of
contracts revised is nil]

  Accessibility: Not satisfactory [ Percentage of responsive tenders=73%; Women

owned organizations participated= nil; Tender concentration by region and agency are
100% & 53% ]

  Market/suppliers diversification: 100% OTM, 20% e-GP
  Corruption: Not evident [percentage of single tender= 30%; percentage of cases

where single tender was responsive=40%; No. of zero submission=nil]

  Misc.: Re-tendering in 13.33% cases; cancellation in 13.33% cases.

49

3.10  Summary of Findings: Findings elaborated above are summarised below and marked as

compliance and non-compliance.

Compliance

(1)  Proper Maintenance of

Procurement
Documentation

(2)  Preparation of Technical

Specification by Technical
Specification Committee

(3)  Preparation of Official Cost
Estimate (OCE)  by Official
Cost Estimate Committee
(OCEC )

(4)  Proper Advertisement

(5)  Use of proper STD

(6)  Proper Tender Security and
Performance Security
(7)  Proper Tender Submission

Time

(8)  Formation of TOC and

TEC

(9)  Timely issue of  NoA

(10) Contract Award within

stipulated time

(11) Proper Tender Processing

Time

(12) No Cost Overrun

All  documents  relating  to  procurement  were  maintained
properly. In case of e-GP, documents were kept in the archive
of e-GP Portal and some documents were kept in file also. In
case of manual system, all documents were maintained in file
and readily available [Ref. Annex-I, Page 60].
Technical  Specifications  of  goods  were  prepared  by  various
“Technical  Specification  Preparation  Committees”  formed  by
the  Health  Services  Division  (HSD)  consisting  of  various
expert  persons  although  PE  is  authorized  to  prepare  such
specification as per Rule 29 of PPR, 2008.
OCE was prepared by the OCEC formed by the HSD although
PE is authorized to form such committee as per Rule 16(5)(Ka)
of PPR, 2008. However, number of members of the committee
was as per rule [Ref. Table 28, Page 40].
Advertisement  was  made  properly  as  per  Rule  90  of  PPR,
2008 & e-GP Guidelines [Ref. Table 13, Page 20].
Proper  STDs  were  used  as  per  Rule  4  of  PPR,  2008  [Ref.
Annex-I, Page 60].
Tender Security and Performance Security were as per Rule 22
& 27 of PPR, 2008 [Ref. Annex-I, Pages 60-61].
Minimum  days  were  allowed  as  per  61(4)  and  83(1)  of  PPR,
2008 [Ref. Table 19, Page 29].
TOC and TEC were formed by the HSD although these were
supposed  to  be  formed  by  the  HOPE  and  AA  respectively  as
per Rule 7 & 8 of PPR, 2008. However, number of members
of these committees were as per rules. In case of e-GP, e-GP
guidelines was followed [Ref. Table 28, Page 40].
NoA  was  issued  within  1  week  (7  days)  from  the  date  of
approval  of  the  evaluation  report  in  most  of  the  cases  and
within  initial  tender  validity  period  as  per  Rule  102  of  PPR,
2008 [Ref. Table 19, Page 29].
Contract was signed within 28 days of the issue of NOA in all
cases as per Rule 102 of PPR, 2008. Specific contract awards
were sent to the BPPA for publication [Ref. Table 19, P- 29].
“Opening  to  NoA  issue”  process  was  delayed  in  4  contracts
and  “Opening  to  Contract Signing”  process  was  delayed  in  2
contracts  only  as  per  Schedule  III  Part-  ka  under  Rule  36  of
PPR,  2008.  It  indicates  pursue  of    timeliness  in  contract
processing [Ref. Table 19, page 29].
Original  Contract  Price  and  Actual  Contract  Price  were  same
in all cases. It means no cost variation [Ref. Table 24, P-34].

50

(13) Verification of Goods

A  committee  named  “Technical  Inspection  and  Acceptance
Committee” has been formed by the HSD consisting of expert
persons of various agencies to verify the quantity and quality
of  goods  according  to  specification  as  per  Rule  40  of  PPR,
2008. All goods were verified by the committee. Some goods
were
in  Drug  Administration  Laboratory  before
verification of the committee [Ref. Annex-I, Page 61].

tested

(14) Minimum Audit Objection   Audit  completed  for  19  contracts  out  of  which  only  one  has
objection  and  that  objection  was  not  serious  in  nature  [Ref.
Table 26, Page 37].

(15) No Complaints and Appeal   No  contract  disputes  were  raised  and  no  complaints  and
appeals were lodged against any contract [Ref.Annex-I, P- 59].

Non-compliance/deficiency

(16) Limited Procurement

Management Capacity

(17) Non-existence of

Procurement Cell
(18) Annual Procurement

Plan(APP) not properly
made

(19) Inconsistence in

Procurement Method,
Type, System, Package

(20) Following of ICT instead of

NCT

(21) Delay in Tender Evaluation

in Some Cases

(22) Lack of DoFP: Approval of

All Evaluation Reports by
the HOPE

63%  of  manpower  was  vacant  (out  of  202  officers  &  staff,
only  75  were  working).  Workload  was  heavy  (68  contracts
signed in FY 2022-23 & 2023-24) compared to the manpower
[Ref. Table 10, Page 16].
No Procurement Cell existed in CMSD. So the staff could not
be imparted training on procurement.
APP was not prepared as per Rule 16(5) of PPR, 2008 in the
beginning  of  each  financial  year  by  the  PE.  CMSD  after
getting requisition from the LD Office, prepared APP for each
package  and  got  it  approved  by  the  HOPE.  No  proof  of
sending specific APP to the BPPA for publication in BPPA‟s
website [Ref. Annex-I, 1.1, Page 55].
Procurement  method  was  not  mentioned  in  OPs  and  APPs.
Procurement type was mentioned as NCB/ICB in OPs. In APP,
NCT  or  ICT  was  mentioned  and
tender  was  floated
accordingly. OTM was followed in all 30 contracts (28 goods
&  2  non-consulting  services)  although  in  some  cases  other
methods  could  have  been  followed  in  order  to  save  time  and
cost. Packages mentioned in APP were not in consistence with
the packages mentioned in OPs [Ref. Table 27, Page 39].
ICT  was  followed  in  22  contracts  but  no  international
tenderers submitted tenders. All tenderers were domestic. But
much time and efforts were spent for ICT[Ref.Table 13,P- 20].
Tender  evaluation  was  done  as  per  Rule  98  &  100  of  PPR,
2008. But in some cases timetable mentioned in the Schedule
III  Part-  ka  under  Rule  36  of  PPR,  2008  was  not  abided  by
(delay occurred in evaluation of 11 contracts) [Ref. Table 19 &
20, Pages 29-30].
All evaluation reports were approved by the HOPE i.e. DoFP
was not followed as per Rule 36 of PPR, 2008 [Ref. Annex-I,
6.8, Page 100 ].

51

(23) Low Competition in
Procurement

(24) Lower Quoted Price

(25) Tender concentration

(26) Time Overrun

(27) Delay in Distribution of

goods

(28) Delay in Bill Payment

(29) Quality of some goods not

up to the mark

Single tender document was sold in 4 contracts. In 9 contracts
single  tender  was  submitted  and  in  13  contracts  number  of
tender submission was less than 3. Re-invitation was made in 4
contracts  only.  In  12  contracts  single  tender  was  responsive.
Although these are not unlawful but these indicate the lack of
competitiveness which is contrary to the objective of the PPR,
2008.  However,  low  participation  could  not  be  attributed  to
improper  publication  of  advertisement  and  pre-bid  meeting
[Ref. Table 12,13,14 &16, Pages 18-24].
Quoted  Price  was  below  the  OCE  in  all  cases.  But  in  some
cases  QP  was  much  below.  8  Contracts  had  QP  below  more
than 30% of the OCE out of which 4 contracts had more than
40% below. This indicates that the preparation of OCE without
taking  into  consideration  the  market  price  or  OCE  was
prepared for higher brand item but offer was lower brand item
[Ref. Table 12 & 15, Pages 18 & 24].
16  contracts  were  awarded  to  5  suppliers  out  of  which  1
supplier  (GEMESCO)  was  awarded  with  6  contracts,  1
supplier with 4 contracts and 3 suppliers with 2 contracts each.
The  rest  14  contracts  were  awarded  to  14  different  suppliers.
So  one  or  more  tenderers  winning  a  disproportionate  number
of contracts was frequent [Ref. Table 17, Page 25].
Time overrun was rampant in case of ICT contracts. Out of 22
ICT  contracts  12  had  time  overrun.  This  was  due  to  delay  in
PSI  and  Shipment.  In  case  of  NCT  contracts  only  3  out  of  8
had time overrun. In 10 contracts, LD was imposed (2 NCT &
8 ICT) and in 5 contracts (1 NCT & 4 ICT) time extension was
approved by the authority as per Rules 38 & 40 of PPR, 2008
[Ref. Table 22 & 23, Pages 31-33].
Delay was occurred in distribution of goods. In some cases it
was  abnormal  which  had  adverse  impact  on the  effectiveness
of goods. For example, Radiant Warmer (package no.G-2210)
was  received  on  13-11-23  but  not  yet  distributed  [Ref.  Table
29, Page 41].
Bill  payment  in  many  cases  were  delayed  and  in  some  cases
delay  occurred  without  any  valid  reason.  For  example,  30%
payment of goods received on 22-11-23 has not yet been made
in  case  of  package  no.  GD-2208  (ECG  Machine) [Ref. Table
25, Page 35].
Concerned  Officials  who were  using  the  goods  informed  that
the  goods  were  functioning.  But  they  said  that  the  quality  of
some  goods  especially  equipments  were  not  up  to  the  mark.
Procurement  of  lower  priced  equipments  and  delay  in
distribution  might  cause  some  equipments  to  perform  low
[Ref. Table 30, Page 43].

52

Chapter -Four

Recommendations and Conclusion

4.1 Recommendations

Based  on  the  findings  stated  in  the  previous  chapter,  the  following  recommendations  are  put
forward  for  proper  execution  and  management  of  procurement  contracts  in  CMSD.  The  non-
compliance part of the summary of findings contains the basis of such recommendations.

1.  Enhancing  the  Procurement
Management  Capacity  of
CMSD.

:  Vacant  posts  of  CMSD  should  be  filled  up  as  soon  as
training  on  procurement  should  be
possible.  Internal
imparted to the staff working in procurement sections [Ref.
Findings 3.10(16), Page-51].

2.  Establishment

Procurement Cell.

of

:  A  Procurement  Cell  should  be  set  up  with  the  persons
having  procurement  training.  CMSD  could  follow  LGED,
DPHE  or  BWDB  in  this  regard  [Ref.  Findings  3.10(17),
Page-51].

3.  Preparation

of

proper
Annual  Procurement  Plan
(APP)  as  per  Rule  16(5)(7-
11) of PPR, 2008.

4.  Preparation

Proper
Technical  Specification  as
per Rule 29 of PPR, 2008.

of

5.  Ensuring

Proper
Procurement  Method  &
Package.

6.  Following  of  NCT  instead  of

ICT.

7.  Formation  of  OCEC,  TOC
& TEC by proper authority.

8.  Ensuring  Time  Compliance

in Tender Evaluation.

9.  Following of DoFP.

:  PE should prepare and update the procurement plan in light
of  Rule  16(5)(7-11)  of  PPR,  2008  [Ref.  Findings  3.10(18),
Page-51].

:  Technical specification should be prepared as per Rule 29 of
PPR,  2008  in  such  a  way  that  low  grade  and  low  priced
products are not offered [Ref. Findings 3.10(2), Page-50].
:  Procurement method should be mentioned in OPs and APPs.
Packages mentioned in APP should be  in consistence with
the  packages  mentioned  in  OPs  and  in  case  of  splitting  of
package approval from HoPE or Authorized Officers should
be  taken  as  per  Rule  17  of  PPR,  2008  [Ref.  Findings
3.10(19), Page-51].

:  As  all  suppliers  are  domestic,  NCT  could  be  followed
instead of ICT. It will save time, costs and efforts as well as
foreign currency [Ref. Findings  3.10(20), Page-51].

:  OCEC,  TOC  &  TEC    should  be  formed  and  approved  by
proper  authority  as  per  Rule  16(5ka),  7  &  8  of  PPR,  2008.
Director, CMSD need not be made Chair of all committees
[Ref. Findings  3.10(3)& 3.10(8), Page-50].

:  Time  mentioned  in  Schedule  III  Part-  ka  of  PPR,  2008
should  be  followed  in  evaluation  [Ref.  Findings    3.10(21),
Page-51].

:  DoFP should be followed in case of approval of evaluation
report. All evaluation reports should not be approved by the
HOPE. Some could be approved by the LD especially when
Director, CMSD is the LD [Ref. Findings 3.10(22), P-51].

53

10. Ensuring  Time  Compliance
in case of  issue of  NoA.

11. Ensuring  Fair  Competition

in Procurement.

12. Quoted  Price

commensurate  with
OCE.

should  be
the

:  NoA  should  be  issued    within  7  days  from  the  date  of
approval  of  the  evaluation  report  as  per  Rule  102  of  PPR,
2008.  No  delay  should  be  made  [Ref.  Findings  3.10(9),
Page- 50].

:  PE  should  find  out  the  cause  and  barriers  for  low
participation  and  take  necessary  steps  to  ensure  fair
competition. In case of lack of fair competition PE can take
action  as  per  Rule  33(2)(kha)  of  PPR,  2008  [Ref.  Findings
3.10(23), Page-52].

:  PE  should  find  out  the  reason  of  too  much  below  QP  and
ensure  that  OCE  is  prepared  taking  into  consideration  the
market price as per Rule 16(5ka) and evaluation is done as
per  Rule  98(23)  of  PPR,  2008  [Ref.  Findings    3.10(24),
Page-52].

13. Discouraging

Tender

:  PE  should  ensure  that  tenders  are  not  concentrated  to  few

Concentration.

suppliers [Ref. Findings 3.10(25), Page-52].

14. Discouraging Time Overrun.  :  Time  extension  should  be  discouraged  and  should  be
allowed  in  special  circumstances  based  on  valid  reason  as
per  Rule  40(3ka)  of  PPR,  2008  [Ref.  Findings  3.10(26),
Page-52].

15. Ensuring

Proper

Verification of Goods.

16. Ensuring

Prompt

Distribution of Goods.

:  “Technical  Inspection  and  Acceptance  Committee  (Survey
Committee)” should ensure that products are up to the mark
and  low  grade  products  have  not  been  received  [Ref.
Findings 3.10(27), Page-52].

:  Too  much  delay  should  not  be  made  in  distribution  of
goods. PE should liaise with the LDs to minimize such delay
[Ref. Findings 3.10(27), Page-52].

17. Timeliness in Bill Payment.

:  Bill  payment  should  be  made  timely.  Abnormal  delay

should be avoided [Ref. Findings 3.10(28), Page-52].

4.2 Conclusion

As per provisions of PPA, 2006, PPR, 2008, e-GP Guidelines, 2011 and ToR of the BPPA, the IC
has  carried  out  this  procurement  post  review.  This  review  has  unearthed  some  deficiencies  and
non-compliance of the relevant provisions of PPR, 2008 & e-GP Guidelines, 2011. During review
it  appeared  that,  those  deficiencies  and  non-compliance  were  not  fully  intentional  and  to  some
extent  due  to  unawareness  of  relevant  provisions  of  rules  and  even  for  shortage  of  manpower
comparing  to  the  huge  workload.  It  is  expected  that  the  findings  and  recommendations  put
forward in this report would  guide the CMSD to overcome these deficiencies.

the report.

is  any  kind  of  mentioning  of  the  PPA,
2006  and  PPR,  2008,  there  should  be
mentioning with the year.

low  quoted  price:  There
should be good enough scrutiny regarding
the abnormally low quoted price.
The  use  of  passive  voice:  The  report
should  be  in  passive  voice,  since  this  is
norm in any official document.

Title font problem: The font of the title in
case  of    Chapter  2-  upto  table  5  is  ok,
after that the title font is problematic that
should  be  corrected.  The  Title  should  be
in bigger font.
